Preface
This report is a master thesis project of 35 ECTS, which was prepared at the De-
partment of Environmental Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark in
fulfilment of the requirements for acquiring a Master Degree in Environmental Engi-
neering. The MSc thesis project was conducted by Erica Spadavecchia at Danmarks
Tekniske Universitet (DTU) from February 2019 to July 2019, under the supervi-
sion of the Assistant Professor Valentina Bisinella and the Senior Researcher Anders
Damgaard.
The topic of this study is the solid waste management system for industrial textile
waste for the Veneto region in Northern Italy.
The document comprises of six chapters. Chapter 1 is an introductory to the topic
of the thesis. Chapter 2 provides background information and the objective of the
study.
Chapter 3 describes the methodologies used to carry out the project, and
Chapter 4 presents the results of these analyses. Chapter 5 provides an interpretation
of the obtained results.
Finally, Chapter 6 presents the conclusions of this study.
Additionally, the Appendices contain detailed information and results.
Kongens Lyngby, July 28, 2019
Erica Spadavecchia (s171990)

==PAGE:5==
Abstract
The textile and clothing industry is one of the most polluting manufacture sectors,
and it is accountable for significant environmental burdens. In light of the Circular
Economy targets for source segregation of textile waste in Europe by 2025, factors
affecting the sustainability of alternative management schemes need to be evaluated
with scenarios for the management of textile waste in Europe.
This study assessed the waste management system of pre-consumer textile waste
in the Veneto region in Northern Italy and compared it with improved scenarios imple-
menting textile recycling. The methodologies developed for waste Life Cycle Assess-
ment were applied, while EASETECH was the software where the systems were mod-
elled. The consequential modelling framework with system expansion was followed,
and the reference flow of 1 tonne of pre-consumer textile has been defined to enable
the scale-up of the results.
The LCA investigated four alternative waste manage-
ment scenarios for recycling separately collected cotton, nylon, polyester and blended
fibre 50% cotton and 50% polyester textile waste fibres. Finally, the “best-case” sce-
nario where all the fibres are ideally separately collected into different fractions and
recycled simultaneously. Data for the modelling were retrieved from governmental
sources, private contacts, and literature, although default processes were modelled
with EASETECH or downloaded from Ecoinvent and were improved to fit the ac-
tual technologies. The characterised and normalised results were obtained using the
method recommended from the ILCD.
These results show that the current management of pre-consumer textile waste
is connected to environmental burdens for its disposal. With the available data and
technologies, source segregation and recycling can be beneficial for most fibres except
for polyester. Moreover, the ”best-case” scenario results, suggest that waste manage-
ment strategies for pre-consumer textile waste should focus on source segregating and
recycling selected fibres. Combined sensitivity and uncertainties analyses allowed to
identify the parameters affecting the most the sustainability of the scenarios. The
sorting efficiency and the ratio of substitution, which expresses the avoided produc-
tion of virgin products, are the most influential factors for all the fibres systems.
Based on the results, the polyester recycling process is the only technology producing
environmental loads and further investigations might be required to assess the influ-
ence of methanol and toluene solvents on the overall sustainability of the scenario.
The distances travelled have a significant impact on the environmental profile of the
scenario, and they closely depend on the efficiency of the sorting process.

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
Textile and clothing industries represent a vital manufacture sector in the European
Union and the rest of the world, and compared to the other major industrial sectors, is
second for size and importance only to information technology and tourism (Shishoo
2012). Concurrently, textile and clothing industries are one of the most polluting
industries (Gray 2017) and are associated with a wide range of environmental and
social impacts along the value chain and mainly related to the production and the
processing of raw fibres (Sajn 2019).
Among the natural fibres, cotton is the most common, and it accounts for more
than 90% of the production (Yu 2015). A considerable amount of water both from
precipitation and from irrigation system is required for its cultivation and estimations
report that 2.6% of global water use is destined to cotton production causing drought
in cultivation areas while the damage to the water environment associated with it
is 17% (Esteve-Turrillas and Guardia 2017). The cotton plant is highly sensitive to
fungi and insect attacks, and it requires the application of pesticides and fungicides
along with synthetic fertilisers to improve the poor uptake efficiency.
The whole
manufacture process requires high volumes of water, and it is highly energy demand-
ing (Pensupa et al. 2017), although the adverse environmental effects correlated with
it are chiefly due to the cotton dyeing. Moreover, high volumes of wastewater are
produced with harmful impacts on continental waters especially regarding toxic dyes
(Zhang et al. 2015).
Negative environmental effects are also associated with polyester and synthetic
fibres production. A massive amount of crude oil and energy are required throughout
the production processes as a considerable volume of different chemicals. Particular
concerns are reserved for the emission of volatile organic compound, specifically ac-
etaldehyde and dioxins hence they pose a threat to human health and also to the
ozone layer (Chen and Burns 2006).
Nowadays, global textile demand is steadily increasing, and textile represents one
of the largest growing streams of waste and is forecast to grow. This trend is likely
to continue maintained by population growth, economic development, fashion cycles,
and notably the new phenomenon of ”fast fashion”. Globalisation coupled with con-
sumers needs leads to a radical transition from the luxury fashion industry to the
”street fashion” also called ”fast fashion” which peculiar business strategy is based
on smaller but more frequent collections and lower prices for apparel (Turker and
Altuntas 2014) which makes the consumers willing to buy more items.
==PAGE:10==
2
1 Introduction
Industries and consumers awareness on the negative environmental and social im-
pacts related to the linear trend, take - make - dispose, that characterise the textile
sector is increasing.
However, the major efforts are made to improve the current
system rather than focusing on the redesign of the textile industry following the prin-
ciples of the circular economy. The adoption of a circular mindset for the textile
economy allows a systemic and upstream approach to involve all the relevant as-
pects as product design, manufacture processes, consumption and waste generation
to tackle problems from their root causes.
Legislative bodies in Europe are working to support the transition toward a circu-
lar economy, foster sustainable economic growth, and boost global competitiveness.
In 2015, European Commission adopted an action plan which sets out measures and
targets to ”close the loop” of product lifecycles focusing of production, consumption,
and waste management highlighting the importance of secondary materials markets.
The action plan recognises that the implementation of the European Strategy for
Plastics in a Circular Economy mindset and holistic approach could lead to benefit,
whether applied to other highly environmentally impactful sectors as textile. In 2018,
a revised legislative framework on waste was issued, and targets were set up to pursue
waste reduction and to achieve a credible and long-term path for waste management
and recycling. One of the primary target it the set up of separate waste collection
schemes for textile waste by 2025.
The dysfunctional nature of the linear economy can not be longer ignored by
textile and clothing industries which have to work to ease the transformation into a
circular economy. On the other side, consumers awareness on the disruptive effects
due to the fast fashion phenomenon can no be longer underestimated, and a more
conscientious behaviour have to be adopted buying fewer products with higher quality
and supporting the shift into a more sustainable slow fashion consumption trend.
==PAGE:11==
CHAPTER 2
Background
2.1
Textile and Clothing Industry in Europe
Textile and clothing industry plays a vital role in the European industrial scenario, as
reported by Eurostat in 2015, it accounted for 2.1% of the production value and 5.2%
of employment (Giannini, Iacobucci, and Perugini 2019). Textile manufacture sector
is heterogeneous and covers the entire chain for the production and transformation
of natural and synthetic fibres into the final products, including cloths, industrial
textiles, garments and household goods.
The five most populous countries, Italy, Germany, Spain, France and the United
Kingdom are the biggest producers of textiles, and they account for more than three-
quarters of the total European’s production. Specifically, Italy plays a leading role
in the European textile and fashion industry sector having in its territory 27% of the
companies which generate 30.9% of the turnover (Marenzi 2016). The main contrib-
utors to textile production and remarkably to technical textile manufacture are the
northern countries, like Germany, Netherlands, Austria, United Kingdom, Belgium
and Sweden. The clothing production is dominated by southern countries, mainly
Italy and Portugal and to a lesser extent, France, Spain and the new EU countries of
Bulgaria, Poland and Romania. 20% of the clothing and European textile production
is sold outside the EU regardless of the restrict access to several non-EU markets
(European Commission: Internal Market and SMEs 2019).
Unfortunately, the textile and clothing sector is not only one of the essential indus-
trial reality but also one of the most polluting and harmful to the environment. On of
the primary source of the significant environmental issues is the modern consumption
pattern aggravated by the fast fashion phenomenon, which has led to an exponential
growth of purchases. Between 2000 and 2014, an average consumer raised by 60%
the number of apparel items bought every year (Koszewska 2018). The natural con-
sequence of this new consumption pattern and of the linear trend characterizing the
economy is a steadily increasing demand for new textiles and the design of products
with poorer appearance retention, durability and dimensional stability. The increased
demand for textile supports the increase in the demand for new fibres and therefore,
the escalation in the generation of textile waste.
==PAGE:12==
4
2 Background
2.2
Textile Waste
In 2016, in the European Union, 2,190,000 tonnes of textile waste (Eurostat 2019b)
have been produced and gathered data from EU reports that textile waste represents
0.2% of the total waste generated and it forecast to grow in the future (Eurostat
2019a).
2.2.1
Textile Waste Classification
The EU compiled the List of Waste to provide a common terminology to facilitate
waste definition, classification and waste management practices.
Codes for waste
generated from textile industry are reported into Chapter Four: ”Waste from leather,
fur and textile industries” and are depicted in Table 2.1.
Table 2.1: European Waste Catalogue Codes for Textile Waste (* = Hazardous
waste).

04 02 99
Wastes not otherwise specified
The generation of solid waste is a common problem for both natural and synthetic
fibres manufacture, and they arise during all the production steps, and the disposal of
textile and apparel whenever they reach their end of life. Textile waste is composed of
different fibrous materials made of synthetic or natural fibers. Moreover, textiles are
often engineered to improve their features, and frequently different type of polymers
are blended with other materials to form new and more complex articles. Due to
this complexity, textile waste is classified into ”pre-consumers” or ”industrial” and
”post-consumers” textile waste according to the life cycle stage in which it is produced.
==PAGE:13==
2.3 Textile Waste in Italy
5
The first category refers to materials discarded before being ready for consumers use.
As the adjective industrial suggests, the waste is generated during the manufacturing
processes of fibres materials, and it is characterized by either single polymers whether
it is produced in the early stages or blended fibres systems if occurred in the last
stages. On the other hand, fibrous materials discarded at the end of their service
life are classified as post-consumer textile waste and represent a more complex waste
category due to the complexity and variety of the composition.
2.3
Textile Waste in Italy
Italy is a parliamentary republic of south-central Europe with around 60 million in-
habitants, and it is subdivided into 20 regions, 5 of them under a special autonomous
status which allows them to pass legislation on local matters. In 2018, Italy had the
ninth´s biggest economy in the world and the third in the Euro-Zone if the United
Kingdom is not accounted (Fund 2019). The state is economically divided into two
different areas, a developed and highly industrialized northern part where it is pro-
duced almost 75% of the national wealth and a more agricultural depended and less
developed southern part. The Italian economy relies mainly on the service and indus-
try sectors. The service sector is responsible for the production of three-quarter of
the national GDP (Gross Domestic Product), and it employs around 65% of the total
workforce. The industrial sector is primarily based on SMEs (Small and Medium-size
Enterprises), which are typically family-owned and specialized in the production of
high-quality goods. The manufacturing sector accounts for one-quarter of the na-
tional GDP and it employs 30% of the workforce (ISTAT 2018). The textiles, apparel
and leather sector plays a crucial role in the industrial scene, in 2015, 62.2% of the
turnover produced by the sector was generated by the clothing and fashion branch
while the 37,8% by the textile (Marenzi 2016).
Alongside with the relevance in textile and clothing production, Italy in Europe
has a leading role also in the generation of textile waste as it is displayed in Table 2.2.
Table 2.2: Total textile waste from all NACE activities + households (Eurostat
2019a).

Furthermore, Italy is the first producer of pre-consumer textile waste and as pre-
sented in Table 2.3 it contributes with more than 40% to the total amount generated
in Europe.

Nevertheless, data on the entire generation of industrial textile waste in Italy are
scares for two main reasons: firstly as there is uncertainty on data for industrial waste
and secondly because industrial waste is often assimilated to household waste. The
national production of industrial waste is quantified by ISPRA (The Italian Institute
for Environmental Protection and Research) through the information contained in
the MUD (Environmental Declaration Form) database in which are reported data
relative to the annual declaration drawn up following the Italian legislation. Despite
this, in the last ISPRA waste report has been stated that for the textile and clothing
industrial sector, the MUD coverage rate is remarkably low, below 10%. This trend is
mainly influenced by the presence of many small and medium-size enterprises which
are exempted from the obligation to fill out the environmental declaration form. To
gain more comprehensive knowledge on the waste amount, data contained in the
MUD database are integrated with estimation carried out by ISTAT (Italian Na-
tional Institute of Statistics) made through the application of specific methodology.
Nevertheless, the data are still an underestimation of the reality (Alessandra Vaccari1
2017).
2.3.1
Textile Waste in Veneto
The Italian manufacturing sector is regionalised. The northwest also called ”Indus-
trial Triangle” (Milan - Turin - Genoa), is the old industrial pole and it is characterised
by a large and modern group of industries specialised in machinery, aerospace, naval
and automotive production. On the other hand, the industrial sector developed in the
Northeast and to a lesser extent in the centre, previously an agricultural and rural
area, is typified by high craftsmanship but low technology enterprises specialised in
textiles, clothing, footwear, machine tools, jewellery and furniture.
One of the most industrialised region in the north-east is Veneto, and in its terri-
tory, there are two of the leading textile and clothing districts: the district of Treviso
mainly focused on clothing and the district of Vicenza famous for the leather manu-
facture. In 2016, the amount of pre-consumer textile waste generated in the region is
reported in Table 2.4.

2.3.2
Textile Waste Management
Europe legislative bodies have been working to minimize adverse impacts of waste
while maximizing the positive impacts of proper waste management promoting the
role of individuals, households, businesses, local and national governments (Commis-
sion 2010). EU waste management policies, as the Waste Framework Board, which
represents the waste policies foundation, have been imposed and evolved over the
past 30 years. The objectives were to define a harmonic regulation to prevent and to
reduce the waste generation and consequently to decrease environmental and health
impacts and rising resources efficiency. In 2008, the EU issued the Waste Framework
Directive, which brought a modern perception of waste, from an unwanted load to a
valuable secondary raw material and introducing the distinction between waste and
by-products (Commission June 2016). The Directive delineated the basic principle for
waste management; it demands waste management practices which do not threaten
human health and the environment, which not provoke nuisance and which not endan-
ger places of particular interest and countryside. Finally, EU Member State waste
management legislation shall adopt as priority order the five-step waste management
hierarchy represented in Figure 2.1.
Figure 2.1: Waste management hierarchy.
==PAGE:16==
8
2 Background
According to this new strategy, prevention is the preferred option, followed by
reuse, recycling and other forms of recovery while the disposal as landfilling is the
less preferable alternative. Moreover, in July 2018, the revised version of the legisla-
tive framework on waste had entered into force, and it clearly targets for the reduction
of waste generation and for the establishment of a long term, credible and ambitious
programme for waste management and recycle. Along with all the European Strate-
gies for the adoption of a circular economy mindset, targets and requirements it is
demanded the separate collection of textile waste by the end of 2025 (European Par-
liament and Council 2018).
Every year in the EU, 75% of the collected textile waste is assigned to landfill or
incineration plants, while only 25% is recycled (Ernantez, Barrera, and Navacerrada
2017). Of this, 50% could be suitable for reuse, 25% might be adequate as multi-use
cloths while 30% might be used as a raw material in industrial processes (Ernantez,
Barrera, and Navacerrada 2017). The landfill is the least desirable option for the
management of textile waste. Fibres are not biodegradable, and the energy employed
in the production processes of textile is lost. Incineration allows reclaiming part of
the energy used in the manufacture with an efficiency of 60% (Bartl 2011) and, in
view of the above, it is a more convenient treatment for this waste stream. Recy-
cling faces several issues, and only less than one percent of all materials employed to
produce clothing is recycled back into clothing. This is a consequence of a lack of
sorting technologies for collected clothes and blended fibres and a lack of recycling
technologies.
2.3.3
Textile Recycling
Processes implemented for textile recycling are classified as either mechanical and
chemical methods, and Table 2.5 illustrates the input and output of each recycling
approach.
Table 2.5: Mechanical and Chemical Recycling Input and Output (Le 2018).
Mechanical Recycle
Chemical Recycle
Process
Downcycling

Recycling technologies are usually classified into primary, secondary, tertiary and
quaternary approaches. The first approach consists in the recycling of a product into
its original form, the secondary involves mechanical processes, the tertiary is the py-
rolysis and hydrolysis of polymeric waste to obtain fuel or monomers, and eventually
==PAGE:17==
2.3 Textile Waste in Italy
9
the quaternary is the burning of textile waste to recover the energy content into it.
The mechanical approaches are classified as a secondary recycling scheme as they
generally consist of cutting, shredding and pulling into new fibres that are re-spun
into new yarn or melted and re-extruded or re-blended. Instead, the chemical ap-
proaches are classified as a tertiary recycling technology as they are characterized by
the partial or entire break down of the chemical structure (depolymerization) with
a consecutive re-polymerization or by the melting and dissolution processes through
which the material is extruded into re-usable fibres (Le 2018). Cotton and wool me-
chanical recycling technologies are well defined and established, but the volume is
low, for synthetic fibres and specifically for polyester, the recycling feedstocks mainly
consist of PET plastic bottle. Chemical recycling for synthetic fibres is established
but limited to a few suppliers, while technologies for cellulosic fibre are going towards
the industrial scale-up. Since both these two recycling schemes lead to a loss in the
quality of textiles are defined as down-cycling and their final products are fibres used
as insulation material, wiping cloths or mattress stuffing. Nowadays, researches are
focused on the ”textile-to-textile” recycling practice, which allows maintaining the
quality of the product that is recycled. The pre-consumer textile waste category is
evaluated as it represents a suitable stream for recycling in reason of its simpler com-
position which mainly consist of single fibers and in a less extent of blended materials.
Presently, ”textile-to-textile” recycling may not represent a viable solution consid-
ering that the available technologies are not cost-efficient whether implemented in
full-scale and because recycled fibres are more expensive than virgin products. Ad-
equate technologies to separate and manage the heterogeneous textile waste steam
to allow the characterization, identification and separation of potential constituent
components, dyes, chemicals and fibres blends are the main requirements for future
efficient recycling systems. Finally, textile recycling might not be a viable solution
until it is not less energy demanding, less polluting and less expensive than tradi-
tional production processes for virgin fibres. Efforts need to be made to overcome
technological barriers and to scale-up the processes to commercialize the technologies.
2.3.4
Pre-Consumer Textile Waste Management in Italy
Detailed data on the end of life practises implemented to handle pre-consumer textile
waste are absent or generalised for the total industrial waste flow.
ISPRA 2018
reports that 74.9% of the total Italian industrial waste is recycled, 9% is landfilled
and the remaining part is incinerated, although it is not possible to assume that these
percentages are applicable to industrial textile waste. It is reasonable to suppose that
the main portion of this waste stream is designated to landfill while a small share
is sent to incineration for energy recovery.
This estimation is in accordance with
the data available in Meoni 2016 where is reported the fate of the waste generated
within the textile district of Prato in the region of Toscana, which is on of the most
important in the Italian textile manufacture sector. Accordingly, the current waste
management practises for industrial textile waste in Veneto are assumed to consist of
==PAGE:18==
10
2 Background
90% of the waste landfilled and 10% intended to energy recovery.
2.4
Objectives Statement
The purpose of this work is to apply the LCA methodology to identify those factors
that influencing the sustainability of textile and their end of life will allow minimizing
the impact of textile production through the recycling of textile. The practice of
textile-to-textile recycle is the one chosen for the research as the aim is to obtain a
fibre with properties as similar as possible to the virgin one.
In view of the Circular Economy initiatives and the ambitious target of reaching
the separate collection of textiles by the end of 2025, this study wants to identify on
which textile waste is convenient to focus the attention. Besides, the analysis has the
further objective of assessing which management option for the separately collected
textile waste would provide the most significant environmental benefits.
The geographical area analyzed is Italy as it is the first producer of industrial
textile waste in Europe with a share of 21.2% and specifically it is focused on Veneto
where it is concentrated one of the most relevant textile and clothing industrial reality
of Italy. The waste flow investigated is the pre-consumer textile waste as it has been
identified as the most promising due to the lower degree of physical complexity.
==PAGE:19==
CHAPTER 3
Methodology
3.1
Life Cycle Assessment
Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) is a valuable decision-support tool for industries and
policy makers which allows the identification, quantification and evaluation of the
environmental burdens associated with a product, activity or system, over its entire
life cycle, from raw materials extraction through production and manufacturing, use,
end of life treatment and disposal (IS0 2006). Strengths of LCA are associated to its
comprehensive nature as the implementation of this methodology grants the coverage
of all the environmental impacts associated to the product or product systems that are
constituted by several processes and which is responsible for emissions taking place in
different times and spaces (Hauschild, Rosenbaum, and Olsen 2018). Moreover, the
life cycle thinking perspective adopted prevents practitioners to burden shifting, and
at the same time, it covers a broad spectrum of impacts assigning numbers to facts
and therefore facilitating the comparison of different products and systems (Hauschild,
Rosenbaum, and Olsen 2018). The main limitation is the significant amount of data
that need to be analyzed and evaluated, causing generalizations and simplifications
in the modelling and as a consequence, increasing the uncertainties associated with
the finals results.
LCA methodology development and harmonization have been a process carried out
from the early 1970s and culminated into a series of ISO standards (ISO 14040-14044).
Alongside with the standards, several guidelines and manuals have been issued, and
the most recent and worthy of mentioning is the European ILCD Handbook which
provides more detailed recommendations for the practitioners, for instance, more
data for the impact assessment (Tillman 2010). As reported into either standards
and guidelines, an LCA is an iterative process which comprises of four phases: the
goal and scope definition, the inventory analysis, the impact assessment and the
interpretation as it is represented in Figure 3.1.
==PAGE:20==
12
3 Methodology
Figure 3.1: LCA framework (ISO, 2006).
In the Goal and Scope phases are defined the context of the LCA study, the
reasons why it is carried out, for whom it is performed, the functional unit, the data
quality requirements and the system boundaries are outlined. During the life cycle
inventory, all the direct inputs and outputs related to the production processes are
collected. The aim of the third phase, the life cycle impact assessment, is to translate
the information collected in the second phase into potential environmental impacts
using characterisation factors to assess their contribution. Other two discretionary
steps may be performed. The normalisation could be carried out to assess the relative
magnitude of the impact scores, expressing them through a standard set of reference
impacts (Hauschild, Rosenbaum, and Olsen 2018), while the weighting might be
performed to compare scores among all the impact categories. Finally, in the last
phase, the results of both life cycle impact assessment and inventory are interpreted,
and the sensitivity and uncertainties analysis are carried out to support a correct
evaluation of the results and to define the robustness of the conclusions.
3.1.1
LCA of Solid Waste Management Systems and Modelling
Tools
LCA is an appropriate tool to handle comprehensively and systematically the assess-
ment of the environmental impacts connected to the activities for the management of
solid waste taking into account also the correlation between other economic sectors
(Hauschild, Rosenbaum, and Olsen 2018). Usually, the nature of the LCA study on
waste management systems and on the end-of-life stages of a product, is comparative
and therefore the other life stages are neglected. Particular attention has to be paid
in the definition of the system boundaries, as the waste treatment operations lead to
the production of valuable by-products, for instance, electricity or fertilisers. In the
==PAGE:21==
3.2 Goal and Scope definition
13
case of multi-functionalities, the system boundaries are expanded to account in the
analysis the burdens avoided due to the recovery of resources from waste instead of
producing by conventional means (Björklund, Finnveden, and Roth 2010).
Several tools have been developed to carry out LCA of waste management sys-
tems, such as ORWARE, IWN, WISARD, WRATE and EASETECH (Björklund,
Finnveden, and Roth 2010). Potential discrepancies on the results obtained using
different tools may derive from the dissimilarity on the waste management strategies
adopted in the countries where the tools are developed, or alteration in the intended
users and objectives as on the modelling assumption (Gentil et al. 2010).
The Environmental Assessment System for Environmental Technologies
(EASETECH) is a tool for Waste LCA developed at the Technical University of Den-
mark with the aim of modelling a spectrum of different environmental technologies
adopting a system perspective (Clavreul et al. 2014). The main feature that makes
this software unique compared to others is the use of material fractions for the ref-
erence flow definition instead of original materials. It is obtained a more realistic
representation of the reality, and the fate of each fraction, in the reference flow, can
be tracked throughout all the system (Clavreul et al. 2014). Eventually, it is essen-
tial to emphasise that data present in the software are specific for Denmark and the
other Northern countries conditions and technologies. Nevertheless, users have the
possibility to modify and insert new data or eventually import data from external
database as Ecoinvent.
3.2
Goal and Scope definition
The objective of this study is to evaluate the environmental performance of the cur-
rent waste management system for industrial textile waste in the Italian region of
Veneto and to compare it with improved scenarios implementing recycling technolo-
gies for different fibres. This LCA is limited by the area where it is applied and
analyses the waste stream generated and treated within Veneto disregarding the pro-
duction of textile waste from other industrial sectors and the import from other
regions. The focus of the analysis is on the pre-consumer textile waste while the
post-consumer is neglected. This decision has been made since the industrial stream
present a lower complexity in the composition, and this characteristic makes it more
suitable for textile-to-textile recycling. The decision context identified is specified as
”B: meso/macro-level decision support” since the LCA aims at supporting decisions
regarding future waste management systems and the system modelled is supposed to
affect the present system. A detailed Goal definition is reported in Appendix A.1.
The functional unit of this study is the management of pre-consumer textile waste
generated within the region of Veneto during the 2017/2018 year, in accordance with
the Italian Legislative Decree 152/2006 (Environmental Protection Code) and the
Waste Framework Directive (European Commission, 2008). The amount correspond-
ing to the functional unit is 33140 tonnes. Despite this, the reference flow defined as
one tonne of pre-consumer textile waste to facilitate the scale-up of the results to any
==PAGE:22==
14
3 Methodology
amount is considered significant to analyse. The composition of the industrial waste
generated in the region of Veneto is not available, and it is assumed to be as reported
in Table 3.1. The assumption is based on a case study realised in Turkey by Barışçı
and Öncel 2014.

segregation of textiles at the source. The composition of these impurities stream is
modelled with the EASETECH process for the generation of Municipal Solid ¨Waste
in Southern Europe.
The LCI modelling framework for decision context B is consequential accordingly
with the ISO 14044 standard IS0 2006 and the ILCD guidelines ILCD 2010. Multi-
functionalities are addresses with system expansion and the modelling is performed
with consequential data when available, otherwise attributional to the point of sub-
stitution (average data) are employed. Two different scenarios are defined to perform
this study. In the first one represented in Figure 3.2 is displayed the current waste
management for industrial textile waste in Veneto while in the second one pictured
in Figure 3.3 is shown the expanded and improved waste management system which
incorporate the recycling of textile fibres.

Figure 3.2: System Boundary for the current industrial textile waste management.
==PAGE:24==
16
3 Methodology
Figure 3.3: System Boundary for industrial textile waste management system implementing textile recycling.
==PAGE:25==
3.2 Goal and Scope definition
17
The dashed line represents the boundary between foreground and background sys-
tems. The boxes in the background system symbolize the inputs required for the
processes and their outputs. The dashed arrows represent the avoided production
of virgin materials and energy in the background system, while the black arrows
represent the waste flow. In the current waste management system, the waste flow
generated is collected by private companies and driven to the sorting facility. From
here, 90% of the waste stream is transported to the landfill and 10% to the energy
recovery facility. The waste to energy plant secondary products are electricity, heat,
fly ash and bottom ash. The electricity is recovered and fed in the grid while the heat
is not recovered as there is not district heating in Veneto. The fly ashes are sold to
companies for ”underground reutilization” in exhausted salt mines in Germany, and
the bottom ashes are used to substitutes primary or secondary aggregates. Eventu-
ally, from bottom ashes are also recovered metals like Aluminum and Iron.
The alternative scenario has been built up as the previous one with one main
change. In the sorting facility, the textile fibres segregated at the source are sepa-
rated and are sent to the recycling facility where are treated and spun to obtain a
new fibre. The residues produced during the processes are sent to the energy recov-
ery facility. Transport to each treatment facility is included in both scenarios since
it contributes to the environmental impact of the whole waste management systems.
The study is carried out for the region of Veneto, and thus, data for processes
and technologies are expected to be found for the geographical area of interest. Data
for Italy or countries of south - centre Europe are representative under their climatic
and technological similarity with Veneto. The study is considered to be representa-
tive for five years since waste management technologies, and practices are continually
evolving, and therefore, they can be outdated rapidly.
The study aim is to perform a complete environmental impact assessment of the
pre-consumer textile waste management, and thus, all the impact categories are in-
vestigated. In Table 3.2 are reported the impact categories with their description and
the abbreviations that are used in this study. Firstly, for the impact category ”deple-
tion of abiotic resources” is not used the ILCD version but the CML which splits it
into two different categories: ”Depletion of abiotic resources - fossil” and ”Depletion
of abiotic resources - elements”. An important remark is that the impact categories:
”land use”, ”ionising radiation for ecosystem” and ”resource depletion - water” are
not included in the LCIA method. Finally, the chosen method is not accounting the
”long term” compartment and therefore the emissions occurring in more than 100
years.
A detailed Scope definition is reported in Appendix A.2.

3.3 Scenarios

3.3
Scenarios
The Life Cycle Assessment is carried out analysing seven different scenarios, two base-
lines to evaluate the current waste management system for textile waste in Veneto.
Four different scenarios are built up to study the environmental feasibility of the
recycling technologies for cotton, nylon, polyester and blended fibres containing 50%
cotton and 50% polyester. The analysis is concluded with a best-case scenario con-
ceived to test the hypothesis of all the textile fibres segregated at the source and
sorted to be recycled simultaneously.
3.3.1
Textile Baseline scenario and Textile with Impurities Baseline
scenario
The Textile Baseline Scenario is based on Figure 3.2 and schematized in Figure 3.4.
It is a representation of the current waste management system implemented for in-
dustrial textile waste in the region of Veneto. The composition of the pre-consumer
textile waste is normalised to 100% to simulate the absence of impurities in the waste
stream. The normalized composition is reported in
Table A.4 in Appendix A.3.
Figure 3.4: Textile Baseline Scenario.
The generated waste is collected by private companies with small trucks of 10
tonnes and consuming 0.00907 litres of diesel per kilometre driven, and it is trans-
ported to the sorting facility. Afterwards, the waste is loaded in 28t - 32t Euro5
trucks and 90% of it is sent to the landfill facility of Sommacampagna in Verona and
the remaining 10% to the incineration plant of San Lazzaro in Padua. The current
handling of the waste does not require a sorting process to separate the different fibres
as is not implemented any recycling process, and the waste is landfilled or incinerated.
For this reason, in the baseline scenarios, the sorting facility has the function of a
collection point.
The distances travelled have been calculated as average distances between a col-
lection centre identified for each province in the region of Veneto and the designated
==PAGE:28==
20
3 Methodology
treating facility. The facilities used for the study were selected based either on the
location, the accepted volume and their representativeness. The calculations are re-
ported in Table A.5 and Table A.6 in Appendix A.3. Details regarding the processes
that were modelled on EASETECH are presented in Appendix A.3 Table A.11.
The Textile with Impurities Baseline Scenario is based on Figure 3.2 and schema-
tized in Figure 3.5 which depicts the current waste management system for pre-
consumer textile waste in the region of Veneto as the Textile Baseline scenario.
Figure 3.5: Textile with Impurities Baseline Scenario.
The only difference between the two scenarios is the waste generation since, in this
case, the waste stream contains 94% of textile fibres and 6% of municipal solid waste.
The compositions are reported in Appendix A.2 in Table A.1 and Table A.2. All the
other processes and the parameters are the same as the one in the Textile Baseline
scenario.
The decision of carrying out two distinguished analysis for the current
management system was taken to evaluate how the environmental performances of
the system are affected by the presence of impurities. Details regarding the processes
that were modelled on EASETECH are presented in Appendix A.3 in Table A.12.

3.3.2
Cotton Recycling Scenario
The Cotton Recycling Scenario is based on Figure 3.3 and schematized in Figure 3.6.
Figure 3.6: Cotton Recycling Scenario.
The waste composition, in all the recycling scenarios, is the one reported in the
Appendix A.2 in Table A.1, including both pre-consumer textile fibres and municipal
solid waste as more representative of the reality. The waste flow produced is collected
by private companies as described in Section 3.3.1, and it is driven to a sorting centre.
Within the facility, the cotton fibres are separated with an assumed efficiency of 90%.
The rejected 10% is aggregate to the other waste which is handled in accordance with
the management described in the baseline scenario. The 90% sorting efficiency is
assumed on the basis of expected segregation of the different fibres at the generation
source.
An insight into the mass balance at the facility is reported in detail in
Appendix A.3 in Table A.13. The landfill, the energy recovery plant and the transport
are modelled as in the baseline scenario.
The cotton fibres, previously sorted, are transported into a recycling plant where
they undergo a series of processes to produce high-quality cotton yarn.
There is
no recycling facility in the region of Veneto, and thus, the distances driven from
the sorting facility to the final destination is a significant factor, and it is later on
investigated.
For the modelling, it is assumed a distance of 1 km.
The residues
produced alongside the recycling processes are collected and treated in the energy
recovery facility. Details regarding the processes that were modelled on EASETECH
are presented in Appendix A.3 in Table A.14.

3.3.6
Best Case Scenario
The seventh and last scenario schematized in Figure 3.10 implements the simultane-
ous recycling of all the textile fibres contained in the waste stream. Precisely, the
choice of building up this scenario was made to evaluate the interaction of all the tech-
nologies and the combined environmental performance. The industrial textile waste
generated within the textile districts of Veneto is collected by private companies and
driven to the sorting facility.
In this specific case, all the textile fibres for which
it was identified a recycling technology are sorted and subsequently transported to
the respective recycling plant. The detailed mass balance for the sorting plants is
reported in Appendix A.3 in Table A.22.
The recycling technologies were modelled as in the previous scenarios, and all the
residues are collected and treated at the energy recovery facility. The landfill, the
waste to energy modules and the transport are modelled as in the baseline scenario.

3.4
Life Cycle Inventory
Data for the modelling of the scenarios were obtained from different sources. Spe-
cific information for textile waste and facilities in Veneto were retrieved from ARPAV
2019, and whenever they were not available, on the annual report drawn up by ISPRA
2018. For the Landfill and Energy Recovery modules, the modelling was carried out
with data either found on the official web site of the facilities and based on Susanna
Andreasi Bassi 2019. The research of textile recycling technologies available in the
market allows the identification of mechanisms only for cotton, nylon, polyester and
the blended fibre composed of 50% cotton and 50% polyester. Those have been mod-
elled with information obtained from companies, and whether the sources were not
sufficient, scientific literature was used. The majority of the processes were found in
EASETECH while the remaining were retrieved from the Ecoinvent 3.5 Database.
To provide an accurate representation of the reality, the electricity and heat
sources present in EASETECH were substituted with the electricity and the space
heating mixes of Italy as reported in Table 3.3 and Table 3.4.

The landfill site utilized for this case study is the landfill located in Sommacam-
pagna in Verona. It is modelled based on processes already existing in EASETECH
and changing parameters in accordance with the information obtained from the web-
site of the company operating the facility. It is a landfill for non-hazardous and inert
waste and the little amount of methane produced is not recovered but burned in flares
(HERAmbiente 2019a).
The waste to energy processes are modelled based on the incineration plant of San
Lazzaro in Padua according to information reported in the facility web site (HERAm-
biente 2019b) and data provided by Susanna Andreasi Bassi 2019. Eventually, each
textile fibre in the waste flow was characterized by a specific LHV (Lower Heating
Value). The steam produced in the energy recovery facility is used to supply three
turbines for the production of electricity, which substitutes the electricity from the
grid with an energy efficiency of 24%. The discharged steam is condensed in heat
exchangers which use the water from a nearby canal as a coolant. The condensed
water is reused in the system in the degasser, but there is no heat substitution as
there is no district heating grid in Veneto. Both the bottom ash and the fly ash,
the residues from the Air Pollution Control system, produced in the facility undergo
recovery processes. The fly ashes, after inertization, are employed in ”underground
reutilization” activities to fill exhausted salt mines in Germany. Metals contained
in the bottom ashes, especially aluminium and iron are recycled and substitute the
production of virgin materials while the bottom ashes are treated and successively
used as aggregate for road construction (HestAmbiente 2017).
==PAGE:36==
28
3 Methodology
3.4.1
Cotton Recycling Technology
The cotton recycling plant was modelled following the mechanical recycling technology
implemented in Spain by ”Hilaturas Ferre” and illustrated in Figure 3.11.
Figure 3.11: Cotton Recycling Technology.
Pre-consumer textile cotton clips are meticulously sorted in accordance with their
colour and quality as they are produced from different industrial plants. Subsequently,
the waste is cut into small piece, shredded and eventually it is mildly opened into
fibres without causing irreversible damages to the length of the fibre. In the final
step, an open-end spinning technology allows the production of the cotton yarn with
a yield of 90% (Esteve-Turrillas and Guardia 2017). The residues and the rejects
originated alongside the recycling processes are collected and treated in the energy
recovery facility. The final product is substituting the virgin cotton yarn production
and allow to avoid the environmental impacts associated with the manufacture of
cotton and especially the dyeing step.
Data for the modelling of Cotton Recycling technology are reported in Table 3.5.

3.4.2
Nylon Recycling Technology
The nylon recycling facility was modelled based on the information provided by
the Italian company Aquafil that developed the ”ECONYL Regeneration System”
through which it recovers nylon waste like fishing nets from the oceans and aqua-
culture, fabric scraps from textile manufacturers and carpets destined for landfill
(ECONYL 2019). The recycling plant is located in Slovenia, and it implements a
combined mechanical and chemical technology returning two different final products:
==PAGE:37==

ECONYL FDY yarns on beam and ECONYL texturized yarns on cones. The gen-
eral system boundaries defined by the company are displayed in Appendix A.3 in
Figure A.4, while the recycling technology implemented in the model is outlined in
Figure 3.12.
Figure 3.12: Nylon Recycling Technology.

==PAGE:38==
30
3 Methodology
3.4.3
Polyester Recycling Technology
The recycling processes for polyester was modelled based on the chemical technology
adopted by Patagonia Inc. to treat its polyester waste and illustrated in Figure 3.13.
Figure 3.13: Polyester Recycling Technology.
According to the flow chart, the waste is cut and shredded into smaller pieces until it
is completely broken down into granules. Afterwards, a chemical reaction is applied
to depolymerize the polyester into DMT (dimethyl terephthalate) and EG (ethylene
glycol) which are the intermediates in the synthesis of polyester. DMT is chemically
treated and polymerized to produce new granules of polyester. The last step is the
spinning, which led to the production of the recycled polyester yarn (Zamani 2011).
According to Inc. 2011, the conversion rate from polyester textile waste to DMT is
assumed to be 90%, and this value is assumed as overall efficiency for the recycling
technology. The residues generated alongside the recycling processes are collected and
treated in the energy recovery facility. The recycled polyester yarn is substituting the
virgin material allowing the avoidance of the environmental impacts associated with
the manufacturing process.
Data for the modelling of the Polyester Recycling technology are reported in Table 3.7.

3.4 Life Cycle Inventory

3.4.4
50% Cotton and 50% Polyester Recycling Technology
The recycling technology for blended fibres containing 50% cotton and 50% polyester
is based on the chemical process called Lyocell Process, and it is illustrated in Fig-
ure 3.14. The technology based on Lyocell process dissolve the cellulose, the cotton,
while it keeps the polyester unaltered. The industrial textile waste analysed in this
study is composed of 53% cotton, corresponding to 530 kg and for 16% polyester,
corresponding to 160 kg. In this scenario, it was assumed that 160 kg of polyester
and 160 kg of cotton are blended while the remaining 370 kg of cotton is aggregated
with the other waste stream intended for the landfill and the waste to energy plant.
Figure 3.14: 50% Cotton and 50% Polyester Recycling Technology.
The waste flow is fed into the system and mixed the NMMO (N-methyl morpholine-
N-oxide) solvent, which dissolves the cellulosic fibres . The required amount of NMMO
required is estimated to be 0.05 kg per kg of cellulose in the textile waste stream. The
solution is then pumped through a filter to separate the cellulosic solution from the
polyester which is remained undissolved. The quality of the recovered polyester is
assumed high, and therefore, it is redirected to the spinning process to obtain the new
yarn. On the other hand, the cellulosic solution with the NMMO is then pumped into
showerhead spinner to produce long fibres which are passed through a drying zone to
evaporate the water (Le 2018; Zamani 2011). The residues generated alongside the
recycling processes are collected and treated in the energy recovery facility. The final
products are substituting the virgin cotton and polyester yarn production and allow
to avoid the environmental impacts associated with the manufacture processes.
Data for the modelling of the 50% Polyester and 50% Cotton Recycling Technology
are reported in Table 3.8.

3.5
Sensitivity Analysis
Uncertainties of different types and from different sources are always present in LCA
study, and correct management of them allow to improve and to quantify the ro-
bustness of the results and the precision of the whole study (Rosenbaum, Georgiadis,
and Fantke 2018). Specifically, the sensitivity analysis is a tool to evaluate to which
extent the variation of an assumption or a parameter influences the results of the
model. A system is defined as sensitive toward a parameter if a small change of this
parameter leads to a large change in the results (Hauschild, Rosenbaum, and Olsen
2018).
The sensitivity analysis is subdivided into two distinguished analyses: the pertur-
bation analysis and the scenario analysis. The first step to perform the perturbation
analysis is the selection of the parameters that are tested based on the uncertainties
of the assumption made and on the results of the process contribution analysis. Af-
terwards, each parameter is changed by the fixed amount of 10% and tested one at
the time while the others are left unaltered. For every parameter perturbed a new
set of impact scores is calculated and compared with the initial scores. Ultimately,
the response of the model is evaluated through the calculation of the sensitivity ratio
(SR) which explicit the ratio between the relative change in the score of the impact
category and the change in the parameter. The formula for the calculation of the SR
is reported in Equation 3.1.
SR =
Result2−Result1
Result1
P arameter2−P arameter1
P arameter1
(3.1)
To perform a comparison of the parameters influence, the sensitivity ratios are nor-
==PAGE:41==
3.6 Uncertainty Analysis
33
malised dividing their absolute value with the maximum absolute SR of each impact
category applying the Equation 3.2. In this way, the most sensitive parameter is de-
fined by a normalised sensitivity ratio of 1, and all the others are scaled with respect
to the latter.
nSR =
| SR |
| MaxSR |
(3.2)
The scenario analysis is carried out to test the robustness of the model results in a
different setting than the original, and therefore, a combination of parameter values
and processes are changed at the same time. For each configuration, a new set of
impact scores is calculated and compared with the initial results. The response of the
system is quantified by the relative change calculated applying Equation 3.3.
RelativeChange(%) = (Resultalternative −Resultinitial
Resultinitial
) ∗100%
(3.3)
The model is defined as sensitive to a particular scenario, whether in at least one
of the impact category, there is an absolute value of relative change higher than 50%.
3.6
Uncertainty Analysis
The increased application of LCA to assess the sustainability of different waste man-
agement solutions and to quantify their environmental performances in decision-
making contests has led to the need of balanced interpretation of the results and
the need to ensure transparency and reliability. Nonetheless, due to the complexity
of the modelled systems, the results are subject to uncertainty related with the as-
sumptions made in the modelling phase of the study, the inherent data variability
and unrepresentative datasets (Bisinella et al. 2016). Therefore, the identification
of those factors influencing the outcomes of the study is a fundamental step, and
it should be carried out applying a rigorous approach to integrate sensitivity and
uncertainty analysis. The uncertainty analysis is performed propagating input un-
certainties into output uncertainties with sampling and analytical methods.
The
complexity of LCA for waste managements systems usually does not allow to have a
set of samples for each factor of interest to carry out the analytical uncertainty prop-
agation. A probability distribution is assigned to each parameter and, specifically
the distribution employed in this study is the uniform. It is a function with constant
probability and whose domain is defined by a maximum and a minimum value. For
all the parameters tested in the sensitivity analysis, an uncertainties interval (a,b)
was defined and the input variance for uniform distribution was calculated applying
the Equation 3.4 in which ”b” is the maximum and ”a” is the minimum.
(Vinput) = 1
12 ∗(b −a)
2
(3.4)
==PAGE:42==
34
3 Methodology
For each parameters, the sensitivity coefficient was calculated as reported in Equa-
tion 3.5 where, with i = 1,..,n are identified the factors tested and with j = 1,..,m the
impact categories.
SC
j
i =
(Result2 −Result1)j
(Parameter2 −Parameter1)i
(3.5)
The output variance was eventually calculated with Equation 3.6 where Xi represents
the generic i-th parameter.
Voutput(Xi) = (SC
j
i )2 ∗Vinput(Xi)
(3.6)
To conclude, the total analytic variance for each impact category was approximated
summing up the output variance of each factor. The output variance is used as a
measure of the total uncertainty in the system.
Once concluded with the analytical uncertainty propagation, the Monte Carlo
analysis was carried out. As explained in Bisinella et al. 2016, each parameter is
represented by a variable with a defined probability distribution. The Monte Carlo
tool in EASETECH allows to randomly sample values within the uncertainties prob-
ability distribution interval and to calculate the impact scores. These series of LCA
impact scores are treated in Excel and their statistical properties are calculate.The
variance obtained is the compared with the analytical results to give an estimation
of the analysis robustness.

CHAPTER 4
Results
The results were generated using the EASETECH software which implements the
LCIA method: “ILCD recommended 2013 Prosuit Global NR w/o LT” containing
the impact categories suggested in the ILCD Handbook (EC-JRC 2011) by the Euro-
pean Commission.
4.1
Characterised and Normalised Results
The characterised results for all the scenarios analysed in the study are reported in
Table 4.1 and the detailed overview of the characterised results for each scenario can
be found in Appendix A.4.1, Tables A.24 - A.44. The normalised result are reported
in Table 4.2 and the detailed overview of the normalized results for each scenario can
be found in Appendix A.4.2, Tables A.46 - A.66. For the normalized results, a color
format was applied to show the ranking of the scenarios for each impact category
and to facilitate the identification of the scenario performing better. Green identify a
more preferable situation while red a less preferable one. Negative impact scores are
defined as crediting and correspond to the impacts avoided while the positive values
represent environmental loads.
The normalised results are easier to compare as all the impact categories have the
same units. Based on the results presented in Table 4.2, it is clear that the baseline
scenarios have comparable environmental performances, although the textile scenario
presents lower impact scores because of the absence of the impurities. The 6% of
municipal solid waste in the waste stream, analysed in the textile with impurities
scenario, leads to higher impact because of the presence of an organic fraction which
undergoes biodegradation processes in the landfill with the consequential release of
methane.
The analysis of the normalised results for the recycling scenarios allows spotting
that the polyester recycling scenario is the worst as it reports the highest impact scores
in twelve out of fourteen impact categories. Nevertheless, it is not straightforward
the identification of the scenario performing better in absolute terms.
The best-
case scenario is performing better than the others in eight categories out of fourteen,
but it is not the absolute majority, and thus, it is not correct to assert that is the
environmentally preferable option.

4.2
Process Contribution
The contribution analysis was performed for each scenario with the purpose of iden-
tifying the processes contributing the most to the total impact score of each impact
category. The normalised results were used in the analysis. The processes were ag-
gregated in general categories. The ”Landfill” and ”WTE” processes contain all the
impacts associated with the sub-modules utilised to model the facilities, the ”Trans-
portation” contains the impacts correlated with the transport to landfill, incineration
and recycling plants. The ”Recycling Technology” category aggregates the impacts
of the recycling processes, the spinning step and the substitution of virgin material.
Positive values in the scores represent a negative impact to the environment while on
the other hand, negative values in the scores mean benefit to the system.
In Figure 4.1 and Figure 4.2 are reported the contribution analyses carried out for
the baseline scenarios which present as the unique difference the composition of the
input waste stream. For both scenarios, the Waste to Energy is the most contributing
process to the total environmental crediting while the collection is the most contribut-
ing to the environmental loads of the systems. It is worth mentioning that for the
climate change impact category, the difference between the two scenarios is due to
the Landfill process. In the textile scenario, only textile is landfilled and therefore the
methane production is negligible but in the textile with impurities scenario, a fraction
of organic waste is landfilled, and thus the methane generation is not insignificant
and plays a crucial role in the total score of the impact category.

4.3
Sensitivity Analysis
4.3.1
Perturbation Analysis
The sensitivity analysis was carried out in EASETECH for all the important pa-
rameters introduced in the scenarios. The choice was made based on the results of
the process contribution analyses, and on the uncertainties of the assumptions made
throughout the modelling steps.
As introduced in the Methodology Chapter in Section 3.5, for each parameter its
default value was either increased or decreased of 10% and the system response to
the parameter perturbation was quantified through the calculation of the sensitivity
ratio SR. In this study, the system is considered sensitive to a parameter if the nor-
malised sensitivity coefficient of at least one impact category is over 0.5 (Hauschild,
Rosenbaum, and Olsen 2018). Since, the comparison is performed evaluating the nor-
malised sensitivity ratio (NSR), the most sensitive parameter for each impact category
is represented by an NSR equal to one.
4.3.1.1
Baseline Scenarios
The parameters tested in both baseline scenarios are the same, and they are shown
in Table 4.3.
The decision of evaluating these parameters was taken to test the
uncertainty of the assumptions made in the modelling. Even though the scenarios
are representing the current waste management system for the industrial textile waste
in Veneto, data were not always available, and assumptions were made. There are no
sorting facilities for textile, and the electricity and the efficiency are assumed based on
literature data, and for the fuel consumption in the collection, the process was taken
from EASETECH. Moreover, also the calculations for the distances driven after the
sorting process are based on assumptions.

The sorting efficiency parameter (sort_eff) is the most sensitive for both the base-

the sorting efficiency is increased of 10%, and as a consequence, also the amount of
waste intended for each treatment facilities is modified. Specifically, in the perturbed
scenarios, 99% of the waste stream is sent to landfill, and thus the impact scores are
higher with relative changes over 20% in all the impact categories.
Baseline scenarios are sensitive also to the collection factor (coll) as it presents
a normalised sensitivity ratios higher than 0.5 in the particulate matter and in the
photochemical ozone formation impact categories.

cling technology (p_met), the spinning efficiency for polyester(s_eff_p) are relevant.
The results of the perturbation analysis for the best-case scenario let to pinpoint as
the system is predominantly sensitive to the processes for the cotton and the polyester
which are respectively the fibres to which are associated the highest environmental
benefits and burdens.
4.3.2
Scenario Analysis
The sensitivity analysis is completed performing a scenario analysis which evaluates
the response of the system to discrete changes instead to the variation of one parame-
ter value at the time. Discrete changes could be variation in the system boundaries, in
the technologies or the LCIA methods. The first scenario analysis was performed mod-
ifying the marginal electricity technologies, and the electricity mix in EASETECH
was replaced by the sources reported in Table 4.15.
Table 4.15: Marginal Electricity Mix for Scenario Analysis (Susanna Andreasi Bassi
2019).

All the scenarios were found to be sensitive to a change in the technology used for
the electricity produced in Italy.
The ranking between recycling scenarios does not change with the change in
the electricity mix, although a different behaviour is observed between the system
response for the textile baseline scenarios and the recycling scenarios. The major
changes are seen for the electricity substitution process within the waste to energy
module. The production of electricity from fossil fuel resources leads to more signifi-
cant environmental burdens. However, the benefits associated with the production of
==PAGE:61==
4.3 Sensitivity Analysis
53
electricity from other sources, the WTE plant, instead the fossil resources are much
higher than the benefits connected with the substitution of the electricity produced
from renewable resources. This explains the trends observed in the scenario analysis
results. The baseline scenarios respond to the change in the electricity technologies
with lower impact scores since the saving from the electricity substitution overcomes
the negative scores due to the other processes. On the other hand, for the recycling
scenarios, the electricity change leads to an increase in the impact score of all the
impact categories.

changes between the baseline and the recycling scenarios for climate change, which
is the most sensitive impact category. They are reported into two different graphs as
they have different magnitude in the scores.

For this study, the heat scenario analysis is not meaningful for all the recycling
systems. In the baseline scenarios as in the cotton and nylon recycling scenarios, the
consumption of heat is limited to only one process in the aluminium recovery mod-
ule. Hence, there is no system response to a change in the marginal technologies for
heating space and the relative changes in the impact scores are equal to zero for each
impact category. However, the same does not apply to the other scenario as the recy-
cling technology for polyester, and the blended fibres 50% cotton and 50% polyester
are highly thermal energy demand. The three systems investigated are sensitive, al-
though the ranking between the recycling scenario does not change in response to the
alternative marginal space heat mix.
The analysis was concluded testing the response of the system to the simultaneous
change of the marginal electricity and space heat technologies. The alternative elec-
tricity and the space heat mix are the same as those applied in the previous analysis
and reported in Table 4.15 and Table 4.16. As aforementioned, the combined change
for electricity and space heat is meaningful only for the best-case scenario and for the
scenarios implementing the recycling technologies for polyester and the blended fibre
polyester and cotton. The investigated systems are sensitive to the combined change
of the marginal technologies for electricity and heat, even though the ranking of the
recycling scenarios is unmodified.
4.4
Uncertainty Analysis
The input uncertainty was propagated into output with sampling and analytical meth-
ods. Analytical uncertainty values were calculated with the equations reported in
Section 3.6 for all the parameters once at the time and for all the impact categories,
and it allowed to evaluate the contribution of each parameter to the overall scenario
variance. The Monte Carlo uncertainty propagation was performed with a number
==PAGE:63==
4.4 Uncertainty Analysis
55
of sample N = 10,000 and for all the set of parameters identified in each scenario,
through this method the resulting variance score is a single value which represents
the total parametric variance for the analysed scenario (Bisinella et al. 2016).
Once the output variance was obtained in both cases, then the coefficient of variation
(CV) was calculated with the Equation 4.1
CV J =
√
V (Y )j
Y j
(4.1)
in which
√
V (Y )j is the standard deviation and Yj is the normalized impact score for
the analytical method while it represents the mean of the random samples generated
with the Monte Carlo simulations. Eventually, the analysis was completed defining
the relative change between the output variance defined with the analytical and the
Monte Carlo methods. The relative changes, RC, between the analytical and the
Monte Carlo sampling variance are calculated with the Equation 4.2.
RC = (V arianceMonteCarlo −V arianceanalytical
V arianceanalytical
) ∗100
(4.2)
Generally, it is possible to observe that the relative changes between the variance
obtained with the analytical and the numerical methods are below 10% for the base-
line scenarios, below 20% for the recycling scenario for cotton, nylon and polyester
and eventually, below 30% for the best-case scenario. A different trend is seen for the
50% polyester and 50% cotton recycling scenario in which the relative changes are
much higher in the majority of the impact categories and for the depletion of abiotic
resources elements, it reaches 248%.
The parameters tested and analysed with the analytical method were defined by
different uncertainty range, and therefore, it is relevant to evaluate the importance
of each factor within the impact categories of each scenario. The uncertainty contri-
bution analysis for the parameters included in the recycling scenarios is reported in
Figure 4.9 and Figure 4.10.

4 Results
In the cotton recycling scenario, the total uncertainty is mainly defined by the
cotton substitution ratio (virg_sub), which contributes more than 69% in each im-
pact category. The uncertainties associated with the sorting, recycling and spinning
efficiencies are responsible for the remaining share. As for the cotton scenario, the
total uncertainty for the nylon scenario, in eleven of the fourteen investigated impact
categories, is mainly due to the parameter defining the nylon substitution ratio. The
uncertainty related to the collection factor (coll) is the main contributor for the hu-
man health non-cancer effect impact category with a percentage of 53.13%, and the
uncertainty of the water factor (wat) contributes for 61.97% to the total uncertainty of
the ionising radiation category. The ozone depletion uncertainty is equally controlled
by the uncertainties of the sorting electricity (sort_elec) and efficiency (sort_elec)
factors which are contributing to 40% each.
The total uncertainty in the polyester scenario is predominantly controlled by the
parameter identifying the amount of methanol consumed in the first phase of the
recycling process as solvent (met) which contributes for more than 75% to the overall
uncertainty in more than half of the impact categories. The parameter representing
the amount of toluene (tol) and the sorting efficiency (sort_eff) are mainly respon-
sible for the remaining portions of uncertainty. For the 50% polyester and the 50%
cotton scenario, the uncertainty contribution analysis is not as straightforward as
for the polyester scenario. Generally, the cotton sorting efficiency (sort_eff_c) and
the cotton substitution ratio (virg_sub_c) are the major contributors to the total
uncertainty in each impact category. Nevertheless, the water consumption in the Ly-
ocell process, represented by the parameter ”wat” is controlling with a percentage of
57.37% the uncertainty in the ionising radiation category, and the polyester substitu-
tion ratio (virg_sub_p) is the main responsible for the depletion of abiotic resources
- fossil category total uncertainty.
4.5
Transport Analysis
There are no recycling plants for textile waste in the region of Veneto, and thus, the
transport from the sorting facility to final destination requires a broad investigation.
The transport analysis aims to evaluate whether the distances between the sorting
facility and the different recycling plants could cause a significant change in the envi-
ronmental performance of the scenarios.
The analysis was carried out excluding the polyester recycling in reason of the
magnitude of the impact scores, which are not comparable with those of other sce-
narios. The polyester facility may be located everywhere in Italy and still be the
one performing the worst among all the systems. The first step of the analysis was
the calculation of the characterised impact scores associated with the treatment and
the transport of one tonne of industrial textile waste generated within Veneto. The
==PAGE:67==
4.5 Transport Analysis
59
calculation was performed for different sorting efficiencies and the options tested are
illustrated in Table 4.17.

==PAGE:69==
CHAPTER 5
Discussion
5.1
Contribution Analysis
The characterised and normalised results of the life cycle impact assessment presented
in section 4.1 demonstrate that the baseline scenarios have positive impacts scores and
therefore are responsible for burdens to the environment. The process contribution
analysis allows identifying the collection as the most positively contributing process in
all the impact categories with the only exception of the depletion of abiotic resource -
elements category for which the sorting process is the most contributing. On the other
side, the waste to energy plant aggregate category is the most negatively contributing
process and thus, is leading to the highest environmental benefits. The explanation
for this lays on the fact that the WTE includes the electricity substitution module,
which provides savings as electricity produced from other resources is avoided. The
only significant difference between the two baseline scenarios results concerns the cli-
mate change and the ozone depletion impact categories. The impact scores for the
textile with impurities are higher in consequence of the landfill process. The industrial
textile waste stream is contaminated with general municipal solid waste, hence there
is an organic fraction which in the landfill undergoes biodegradation with the genera-
tion of methane. The substance contribution analysis supports this conclusion, in the
textile with impurities scenario ”Methane, non-fossil” emissions into air contributes
for 79.70% to the total score for the climate change while the ”Methane, dichloro
difluoro-, CFC-12” emissions into air contribute for 75.89% to the total score for the
ozone depletion.
As far as the recycling scenarios are concerned, the results of the life cycle impact
assessment differ according to the textile fibre investigated as the modelled technolo-
gies for the recycle are profoundly different both in terms of energy sources and
chemicals necessary for the processes. As shown in the graphs in section 4.2, the
recycling processes are always the most contributing in all the scenarios.
The process contribution analysis primary outcome is that for all the recycling
scenario, the collection, the recycling process, the spinning and the virgin substitution
modules are the most contributing processes in all the impact category. Investigating
deeper in the recycling technology, the overall negative impact score is due to the
virgin substitution process which creates benefits for the environments in reason of
the avoided impacts related to the avoided production of virgin materials. On the
==PAGE:70==
62
5 Discussion
other hand, the positive contribution is by cause of the recycling process itself. For
the cotton, nylon and 50% polyester and 50% cotton scenarios the environmental
savings due to the avoided production of virgin textile fibres overcomes the negative
impact due to the recycling processes and thus, leading to overall negative impact
scores. To investigate which factors have a major influence on the environmental
performance of each recycling technology, a process contribution analysis was carried
out. The electricity is accountable for the totality of the impacts in cotton recycling
technology since it is a mechanical process which requires only electricity consump-
tion. Figure 5.1 depicts the results of the nylon technology contribution analysis. The
electricity consumption is the process that contributes most followed by the inert filler
materials and the water employed in the process.

==PAGE:72==
